The Argument
I will argue that humanitarian NGOs have come to inhabit a shared social
space. This shared social space produces both the assumptions that are
common across agencies and the debates agencies have with each other
about what it means to be a humanitarian. It is important to understand
the practical logic o
---
 that space as an important aspect o
---
 what is standing between those who give, on the one hand, and "the su
---

---
ering o
---
-the
world," on the other hand; this practical logic mediates e
---

---
orts to help
those in need.
Drawing on in-depth interviews with desk o
---

---
icers.inmanyo
---
thelargest
Western relie
---
 NGOs, I develop two claims about-this space. First, 1 argue
that to understand the practices o
---
 humanitarian relie
---
 agencies we need to
understand that relie
---
 is a 
---
orm o
---
 production and has one primary output ,
or product, which is the "project." Managers produce piojedsandstrive to
make good projects. This has important bene
---
its. Butthepursuito
---
 the good
project develops a logic o
---
 its own that shapes the allocationo
---
resources
and the kind o
---
 activities we see independentlyo
---
 externalinterests butalso
relatively independently o
---
 bene
---
iciaries' needs and pre
---
erences.
Second, agencies produce projects 
---
or a quasi marketinwhich donors
are consumers. The project Is.acommodity, and thus those helped, the bene
---
iciaries, become part o
---
 a commodity. The pursuit o
---
 the good project
encourages agencies to 
---
ocus on short-term results 
---
or selectedbene
---
icranes.
The market in projects also means that bene
---
iciaries are putina position
where they are.in competition with each other to become parto
---
a project.
Considering humanitarian relie
---
 as a modeo
---
governance,itis important to recognize that, in addition to the bene
---
its 
---
orthoseinneedemphasized by liberal observers, and the 
---
orms o
---
 directdomination highlighted
by critics o
---
 humanitarian relie
---
, there is also a
---
orm o
---
indirectdomination at play, which is mediated by the market
---
or projects.
In contrast to accounts that analyze and critique "humanitarian reason" based on a reconstruction that attributes coherence based on ideas
or interests, I argue that the pattern we see is rather one o
---
 a 
---
ragmentation o
---
 reason. In contrast to those who critique international aid as
excessively planned and rationalized/ I argue thatthe current.structure
o
---
 humanitarianism is already shaped by the success o
---
 these critiques,
and the consequences o
---
 this are not only positive ones.

THE MAN WITHOUT QUALITIES

other large organizations. But they also set themselves very

tions need to 
---

there is always a surplus o
---

something that some desk o
---

---

"It is perhaps the most interesting thing about my job," one
desk o

---

the most di

you choose? They are both sick, and you are a medical doctor. And the needs are everywhere, and you will not save the
planet!"

Two Views on International NGOs

Conclusion
I began this chapter by asking how relie
---
 agencies decide where to go,
whom to help, and how. I have suggested that neither values nor interests
determine decision making, nor do needs alone guide decision making.
Values, interests, and. needs cannot by themselves. tell a manager what
to do; they are tndetesmmate-i-that
---

---

---

and taken-
---

they secular relie

---
orm the backdrop o
---

---

---
erent kinds o
---

---
 and are

---

I have suggested that humanitarian relie
---

---
orm o
---

---
orming some things into others. Agencies produce relie
---
 in the 
---
orm o
---

relie
---
 projects. As the unito
---
production is the project, managers seek to
do "good projects." The pursuit o
---

---

own that shapes not only the allocation o
---

---

activities we are likely to see.-and the kind o
---

to see.
When disparities o
---

---
ocus is
o
---

---

cases o
---

---

---

practical logic o
---

---

others, and those who are hardest to help o

I
---

disasters and Violence, we are mistaken. There. is no coverage 
---
or all
a

---

actually providing one. Allocation o

that some o
---

other instead o
---

Conclusion
Populations in need are an end o
---
 humanitarian relie
---
 but also a means to
an end. Managers' relationship to bene
---
iciaries.is not only an instrumental one but it is also an instrumental one. They value the opportunity to
do good and have an impact. But managers also have a projectto deliver.
In the case o
---
 international humanitarian relie
---
 a projectis produced 
---
or
exchange in a market, where it is compared to other projects 
---
rom other
producers on a global scale.
I have tried to bring out a role that populations in need play in humanitarian relie
---
 that is not o
---
ten considered. Populations in need are
part o
---
 the product being promoted and sold in this market, and they
labor 
---
or it.
Much debate about relie
---
 has understandably been about content and
design on the level o
---
 a speci
---
ic intervention. Observers ask:Howrelevant
are services to the a
---

---
ected populations? How e
---

---
icient is an operation?
How participatory are an agency's planning processes?
These discussions certainly have a place. But in addition to such disc
cussions, we also need a discussion about relie
---
 that moves beyond the
content o
---
 relie
---
 toward its 
---
orm. However good the content and design
o
---
 a speci
---
ic intervention, its 
---
orm as a commodity ina global market in
bene
---
iciaries shapes its overall e
---

---
ect. It is a product within a limited range
o
---
 possible products, given consumer pre
---
erences o
---
-those with resources,
and it pits those helped against those not helped.
I will now examine how we got to where we are today. How did populations in need become bene
---
iciaries? One could imagine many di
---

---
erent
histories o
---
 humanitarian relie
---
: one could write this history as a history
o
---

---

the crises that relie
---
 responds to. Or one could imagine this history told as
a history o
---
 the 
---
ortunes and con
---
licts o
---
 the mostprominentrelie
---
 organizations.

The Politics o
---
 Humanitarianism beyond the Politics o
---
 Ideas
Humanitarian relie
---

---

---

a critical analysis o

---

I have argued, however, that we need to look beyond the content o

and the way resources associated with them are allocated. I have argued
that it is not so much anything in particular about the values or meanings o
---

---
the limitations o

 humani6. Habermas already alerted us to the growing distortion o

From Growth to a Focus on the Poor

In the 1950s and 1960s, the 
---
ocus o
---
 international aid was on economic
development and, more particularly, on economic growth as measured
in Gross National Product (GNP). As Martha Finnemore notes in her
study o
---
 the World Bank, "Development during. these years was under"
stood to be increasing GNp, or perhaps GNP per capita."34The hope was
that growth in turn would lead to a higher standard o
---
 living and to less
inequality in developing nations.
The role o
---
 international aid was seen principally as aninitial source o
---

capital. Investments in modern, industrial sectors were thought to trigger
growth and more investment and thus help developing nations "catch
34. Martha Finnemore, "Rede
---
ining Development at the World Bank," inlnternational Development and the Social Sciences: Essays on the History and Politics o
---
 Know/edge, ed. Frederick Cooper and
Randall Packard (Berkeley: University o
---
 Cali
---
ornia Press, 1997), 207.

84

up" with industrialized nations. It is interesting to re
---
lect on the category "modern, industrial sector." According to Erik Thorbecke, "Clearly
the adoption o
---
 GNP growth as both the objective and the yardstick o
---

development was directly related to the conceptual state o
---
 the art in the
1950s. The major theoretical contributions which guided the develop"
ment community during that decade were conceived within a one-sector,
aggregate

The Symbolic Structure o
---
 the Field
Common stakes have brought agencies closer together. Within this more
integrated space, however, there has also been a lot o
---
 debate and disagreement. The expansion o
---
 humanitarianism has created a set o
---
 common stakes, which create both engagement and competition. How can
we make sense o
---
 these divisions?
A common response 
---
rom scholarly observers is. to develop typologies. There are three in
---
luential typologies o
---
 humanitarian relie
---
 organizations: that o
---
 Abby Stoddard, that o
---
 Peter Weiss, and that o
---
 Dennis
Dijkzeul and Markus Moke. Abby Stoddard compares organizations to
determine whether they are Dunantist or Wilsonian,. and whether they
are in 
---
avor o
---
 rule-based coordination or not. Dunantist organizations
seek to position themselves in a neutral space outside the sphere governed by state interests; Wilsonian organizations are more pragmatic and
see their work as compatible with the aims o
---
 Western, and in particular
as comparable with US, 
---
oreign policies. In each o
---
 these two camps, some
agencies are in 
---
avor o
---
 working with other agencies to create a community o
---
 practitioners, and others insist more on their independence
(
---
ig. 4.1).48 Peter Weiss diagnoses a spectrum o
---
 attitudesJrom Classicist
to solidaristic (
---
ig. 4.2).49 Dennis Dijkzeul and Markus Moke draw a.map
according to whether organizations are independent or subcontractors,
impartial or solidaristic (
---
ig. 4.3).50 With this they take Weiss's representation one step 
---
urther by recognizing that there are severaltypes o
---
 departures 
---
rom the classicist view.
Each o
---
 these typologies points to important di
---

---
erences among humanitarian relie
---
 agencies. But they are based on classi
---
ying positions
rather than classi
---
ying the way distinctions between positions are drawn,
and the di
---

---
erences discussed do not exhaust the distinctions drawn
among agencies. They 
---
ocus, 
---
or example, exclusively on secular agencies

Humanitarianism and Politics: Ideas, Field, Practices
There has been much debate about the relationship o
---
 humanitarianism
to politics. Is humanitarianism inherently political? Or are humanitarianism and politics mutually exclusive? ICRC president Claudio Sommaruga gave expression to the second view when he said in

go their separate ways i

politicized somehow. It is a political project in a political world. Its mission is a political one_;_to restrain and ameliorate the use o
---

The Spirit o
---
 Sphere
It is important to bear in mind the idealist side o
---

Sphere sees itsel
---

In this tradition, humanitarians argue that people not only need 
---
ood,
water, health care, and shelter, but that they have a rightto 
---
ood, water,
health care, and shelter. The concept o

be the basis 
---

with, shaping an encounter with bene

than primarily as people in need. The Sphere standards are meant to be
understood as a concretization o

---

parameters

global a

---

In this research design, reliability and Validity depend onthesharedsocialposttion o

The questions I asked targeted: decision-making processes; tacit, practical
knowledge; structures o

Interview research sometimes raises questions about whetherrespondents are
telling the truth and how what the respondentis telling us rdates to the underlying reality. It is important to note that di
---

---
erent kinds o
---
 research design relate
to this problematic in di
---

---
erent ways. I
---
we ask respondents about 
---
acts, values,
or opinions, we 
---
ace a di
---

---
erent challenge than i
---
 We ask about Jlnictices arid sto-

180

ries. Asking about practices and processes does not solve the problem o
---
 validity,
but it does go some way toward addressing it. Interviews about practices create
a set o
---
 engagements between interviewer and respondent that are meaning
---
ul
beyond the 
---
actual and make the researcher less dependent on 
---
actual correctness.
Respondents might lie about theirincome i
---
asked in a survey, so that i
---
 we ask
about income, and that in
---
ormation is 
---
alse, we have not gained much in
---
ormation. Respondents will 
---
ind it harder to make up a whole alternative everyday work
li
---
e with concrete stories. I
---
 we askaboutprocesses and practices, and some 
---
actual
details are omittedor relayed in a.tendentious way, we still have gained valuable
insights.
In that sense, asking 
---
or stories is more similar to direct observation than it is
to survey research or questions about values, ideas, or views, though it may never
reach direct observation's closeness to practice because it takes respondents out
o
---
 the context o
---
their work. We also lose, o
---
 course, in depth compared to ethnographic observation; on the other hand, we gain by being able to obtain insights
across a set o
---
 organizations.

---

to the 
---
indings o

icial presentations o

---

---
ield o

ices.
I also worked in the Lyndon B. Johnson and John E Kennedy presidential archives
to explore the history o
---
